      D E F O G: D EFOGGING D ISCRETE F LOW M ATCHING
002   FOR G RAPH G ENERATION
003

005    Anonymous authors
006    Paper under double-blind review
007
008
009                                               A BSTRACT
010
011            Graph generation is fundamental in diverse scientific applications, due to its
012            ability to reveal the underlying distribution of complex data, and eventually
013            generate new, realistic data points. Despite the success of diffusion models in this
014            domain, those face limitations in sampling efficiency and flexibility, stemming
015            from the tight coupling between the training and sampling stages. To address this,
016
               we propose DeFoG, a novel framework using discrete flow matching for graph
               generation. DeFoG employs a flow-based approach that features an efficient
017
               linear interpolation noising process and a flexible denoising process based on
018
               a continuous-time Markov chain formulation. We leverage an expressive graph
019            transformer and ensure desirable node permutation properties to respect graph
020            symmetry. Crucially, our framework enables a disentangled design of the training
021            and sampling stages, enabling more effective and efficient optimization of model
022            performance. We navigate this design space by introducing several algorithmic
023            improvements that boost the model performance, consistently surpassing existing
024            diffusion models. We also theoretically demonstrate that, for general discrete data,
025            discrete flow models can faithfully replicate the ground truth distribution - a result
026            that naturally extends to graph data and reinforces DeFoG’s foundations. Exten-
027
               sive experiments show that DeFoG achieves state-of-the-art results on synthetic
               and molecular datasets, improving both training and sampling efficiency over dif-
028
               fusion models, and excels in conditional generation on a digital pathology dataset.
029
030
031   1   I NTRODUCTION
032
033   Graph generation has become a fundamental task across diverse fields, from molecular chemistry
034   to social network analysis, due to graphs’ capacity to represent complex relationships and generate
035   novel, realistic structured data. Diffusion-based graph generative models (Niu et al., 2020; Jo et al.,
036   2022), particularly those tailored for discrete data to capture the inherent discreteness of graphs (Vi-
037   gnac et al., 2022; Siraudin et al., 2024), have emerged as a compelling approach, demonstrating
038   state-of-the-art performance in applications such as molecular generation (Irwin et al., 2024),
039
      reaction pathway design (Igashov et al., 2024), neural architecture search (Asthana et al., 2024), and
      combinatorial optimization (Sun & Yang, 2024). However, the sampling processes of these methods
040
      remain highly constrained due to a strong interdependence with training: design choices made
041
      during training mostly determine the options available during sampling. Therefore, optimizing pa-
042   rameters such as noise schedules requires re-training, which incurs significant computational costs.
043
044   Recently, Campbell et al. (2024); Gat et al. (2024) introduced a discrete flow matching (DFM)
045
      framework, which maps noisy to clean discrete data with iterations similar to diffusion models.
      However, DFM has demonstrated superior performance compared to discrete diffusion models
046
      by leveraging a straightforward and efficient noising process, and a flexible formulation based
047
      on continuous-time Markov chain (CTMC) for denoising. Specifically, its noising process in-
048   volves a linear interpolation in the probability space between noisy and clean data, displaying
049   well-established advantages for continuous state spaces in terms of performance (Esser et al., 2024;
050   Lipman et al.) and theoretical properties (Liu et al., 2023). Most notably, the denoising process in
051   DFM uses an adaptable sampling step scheme (both in size and number) and CTMC rate matrices
052   that are independent of the training setup. Such a decoupling allows for the design of sampling
053   algorithms that are disentangled from training, enabling efficient performance optimization at the
      sampling stage without extensive retraining. However, despite its promising results, the applica-


                                                         1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      bility of DFM to graph generation remains unclear, and the proper exploitation of its additional
055   training-sampling flexibility is still an open question in graph settings.
056
057   In this work, we propose DeFoG, a novel and versatile DFM framework for graph generation
058
      that operates under theoretical guarantees, featuring a linear interpolation noising process and
      CTMC-based denoising process inherited from the DFM model. Our contribution begins by
059
      proposing the first discrete flow matching model tailored to graphs, ensuring node permutation
060
      equivariance to respect graph symmetry and addressing the model expressivity limitations inherent
061   to this data modality (Morris et al., 2019). Additionally, to “defog” the design space of this
062   decoupled training-sampling framework, we extend and propose novel training and sampling
063   algorithms, including alternative initial distributions, modifications of the CTMC rate matrices that
064   govern the denoising trajectory, and time-adaptive strategies designed to align with dataset-specific
065   characteristics. We explicitly demonstrate that these algorithmic improvements, which can be
066   seamlessly integrated into training and sampling, accelerate convergence and enhance generation
067   performance by a large margin. To assert DeFoG’s strong theoretical foundation, we extend existing
068   guarantees of DFM models for general discrete data. First, we reinforce the pertinence of the
069
      loss function by explicitly relating it to the model estimation error. Then, we demonstrate that the
      distribution generated by the denoising process in DFM models remains faithful to the ground truth
070
      distribution. Both theorems extend naturally to graph data, providing strong support for the design
071
      choices of our new graph generative framework.
072
073   Our experiments show that DeFoG achieves state-of-the-art performance on both synthetic graph
074   datasets with diverse topologies and complex molecular datasets. Notably, while the vanilla DeFoG
075
      framework performs on par with diffusion models, our tailored training-sampling optimization
      pipeline permits DeFoG to consistently outperform these models. Furthermore, DeFoG achieves
076
      performance comparable to certain diffusion models with only 5 to 10% of the sampling steps,
077
      highlighting significant efficiency gains. We also underscore DeFoG’s versatility through a con-
078   ditional generation task in digital pathology, where it notably outperforms existing unconstrained
079   models. In a nutshell, DeFoG opens the door to more flexible graph generative models that can
080   efficiently handle a wide range of tasks in diverse domains with improved performance and reduced
081   computational costs.
082
083
      2   R ELATED W ORK
084
085   Graph Generative Models Graph generation has applications across various domains, including
086   molecular generation (Mercado et al., 2021), combinatorial optimization (Sun & Yang, 2024), and
087   inverse protein folding (Yi et al., 2024). Existing methods for this task generally fall into two main
088   categories. Autoregressive models progressively grow the graph by inserting nodes and edges (You
089   et al., 2018; Liao et al., 2019). Although these methods offer high flexibility in sampling and facil-
090   itate the integration of domain-specific knowledge (e.g., for molecule generation, Liu et al. (2018)
091
      perform valency checks at each iteration), they suffer from a fundamental drawback: the need to
      learn a node ordering (Kong et al., 2023), or use a predefined node ordering (You et al., 2018) to
092
      avoid the overly large learning space. In contrast, one-shot models circumvent such limitation by
093
      predicting the entire graph in a single step, enabling the straightforward incorporation of node per-
094   mutation equivariance/invariance properties. Examples of these approaches include graph-adapted
095   versions of VAEs (Kipf & Welling, 2016), GANs (De Cao & Kipf, 2018), or normalizing flows (Liu
096   et al., 2019). Among these, diffusion models have gained prominence for their state-of-the-art
097   performance, attributed to their iterative mapping between noise and data distributions.
098
099   Graph Diffusion One of the initial research directions in graph diffusion sought to adapt continu-
100
      ous diffusion frameworks (Sohl-Dickstein et al., 2015; Ho et al., 2020; Song et al., 2020) for graph-
      structured data (Niu et al., 2020; Jo et al., 2022; 2024), which introduced challenges in preserving
101
      the inherent discreteness of graphs. In response, discrete diffusion models (Austin et al., 2021) were
102
      effectively extended to the graph domain (Vignac et al., 2022; Haefeli et al.), utilizing Discrete-Time
103   Markov Chains to model the stochastic diffusion process. However, this method restricts sampling
104   to the discrete time points used during training. To address this limitation, continuous-time discrete
105   diffusion models incorporating CTMCs have emerged (Campbell et al., 2022), and have been
106   recently applied to graph generation (Siraudin et al., 2024; Xu et al., 2024). Despite employing
107   a continuous-time framework, their sampling optimization space remains limited by training-
      dependent design choices, such as fixed rate matrices, which hinders further performance gains.

       Figure 1: Overview of DeFoG. One node x, with 3 potential states {green, yellow, red}, is selected
118
       to illustrate both noising and denoising processes. For noising, DeFoG follows a straight path from
119    the one-hot encoding p1 of the clean node to the initial distribution p0 . For denoising, a network        bility at the next time point. This is applied to all nodes and edges to denoise the entire graph.
123
124    Discrete Flow Matching Flow matching (FM) models emerged as a compelling alternative to
                                                                     t+Δt | x1 )Δt = p(xt+Δt | xt )
                                                      xtn + R*(xtn, xgenerative
125    diffusion models in the realm of iterative refinement         n       n        approaches
                                                                                        n       n   for continuous state
126    spaces (Lipman et al.; Liu et al., 2023). This framework has been empirically shown to enhance
127    performance and efficiency in image generation (Esser et al., 2024; Ma et al., 2024). To address
128    discrete state spaces, such as those found in protein design or text generation, a DFM formulation
129    has been introduced (Campbell et al., 2024; Gat et al., 2024). This DFM approach theoretically
130    streamlines its diffusion counterpart by employing linear interpolation for the mapping from data
131    to the prior distribution. Moreover, its CTMC-based denoising process accommodates a broader
132
       range of rate matrices, which need not be fixed during training. In practice, DFM consistently
       outperforms traditional discrete diffusion models. In this paper, we build on the foundations of
133
       DFM with the aim of further advancing graph generative models.
134
135
       3                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               D ISCRETE F LOW M ATCHING
136
137    In this section, we introduce the DFM framework as originally proposed by Campbell et al. (2024).
138    We adopt their notation for clarity and illustrate it under the graph setting in Figure 1.
139
140    3.1                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              C ONTINUOUS -T IME M ARKOV C HAINS AND D ISCRETE F LOW M ATCHING
141
142    In generative modeling, the primary goal is to generate new data samples from the underlying
143    distribution that produced the original data, pdata . An effective approach is to learn a mapping
144    between a simple distribution pϵ , which is easy to sample from, and pdata . DFM models achieve
145    this mapping via a stochastic process over the time interval t ∈ [0, 1] for variables in discrete
146
       state spaces. The initial distribution, p0 = pϵ , is a predefined noise distribution, while p1 = pdata
       represents the target data distribution.
147
148
       Univariate Case At any time t, we consider a discrete variable with Z possible values, denoted
149
       zt ∈ Z = {1, . . . , Z}. The marginal distribution of zt is represented by the vector pt ∈ ∆Z−1 ,
150    where ∆Z−1 represents the corresponding probability simplex1 . Since handling pt directly is not
151    straightforward, DFM establishes a conditioned noising trajectory from the chosen datapoint z1 to
152    the initial distribution through a simple linear interpolation:
153

154
155    where δ(zt , z1 ) is the Kronecker delta which is 1 when zt = z1 . Importantly, the original marginal
156    distribution can be backtracked via pt (zt ) = Ep1 (z1 ) [pt|1 (zt |z1 )].
157    In the denoising stage, DFM reverses the previous process using a CTMC formulation. In general,
158    a CTMC is characterized by an initial distribution, p0 , and a rate matrix, Rt ∈ RZ×Z that models
159    its evolution across time t ∈ [0, 1]. Specifically, the rate matrix defines the instantaneous transition
160    rates between states, such that:

164   Intuitively, Rt (zt , zt+dt )dt yields the probability that a transition from state zt to state zt+dt will
165
      occur in the next infinitesimal timeP step dt. By definition, we have RtP  (zt , zt+dt ) ≥ 0 for zt ̸= zt+dt .
      Consequently, Rt (zt , zt ) = − zt+dt ̸=zt Rt (zt , zt+dt ) to ensure zt+dt pt+dt|t (zt+dt |zt ) = 1.
166
167
      Under this definition, the marginal distribution and the rate matrix of a CTCM are related by a
      conservation law, the Kolmogorov equation, given by ∂t pt = RtT pt . If expanded, this expression
168
      unveils the time derivative of the marginal distribution as the net balance between the inflow and
169
      outflow of probability mass at that state. When this relation holds, we say that Rt generates pt .
170
171   Since we aim to reverse a z1 -conditional noising process pt|1 (·|z1 ) ∈ ∆Z−1 (recall Eq. (1)), DFM
172   instead considers a z1 -conditional rate matrix, Rt (· , ·|z1 ) ∈ RZ×Z that generates it. Under mild
173   assumptions, Campbell et al. (2024) present a closed-form for a valid conditional rate matrix (though
174   others exist), i.e., that verifies the corresponding Kolmogorov equation, defined as:
175                                           ReLU[∂t pt|1 (zt+dt |z1 ) − ∂t pt|1 (zt |z1 )]
176                 Rt∗ (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) =                                                        for zt ̸= zt+dt ,   (3)
                                                         Zt>0 pt|1 (zt |z1 )
177
178   and Zt>0 = |{zt : pt|1 (zt |z1 ) > 0}|. Again, normalization is performed for the case zt = zt+dt .
179   Intuitively, Rt∗ redistributes probability mass by applying a positive rate to states needing more mass
180
      than the current state zt .

212   4      D ISCRETE F LOW M ATCHING FOR G RAPHS
213
214   In this section, we introduce DeFoG, a novel and flexible DFM framework for graph generation.
215   We first describe the necessary components and desired properties for extending the method to
      graph data. Then, we explore various algorithmic improvements designed to enhance performance.


                                                                  4
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


216
      4.1   A DAPTATION OF DFM TO G RAPHS
217
218   Instead of considering the general multivariate data denoted as z 1:D , we now instantiate undirected
219   graphs with N nodes as a D-dimensional variable. We define the node set x1:n:N = (x(n) )1≤n≤N
220   and the edge set e1:i<j:N = (e(ij) )1≤i<j≤N . A graph corresponds to G = (x1:n:N , e1:i<j:N ), such
221   that x(n) ∈ X = {1, . . . , X}, e(ij) ∈ E = {1, . . . , E}, and D = N + N (N − 1)/2, thus amenable
222   to the DFM framework. However, graphs are not merely joint variables of nodes and edges; they
223   encode complex structures, relationships, and global properties essential for modeling real-world
224   systems, while adhering to specific invariances. To address these graph-specific challenges, we
225   propose DeFoG (Discrete Flow Matching on Graphs), a framework tailored to develop graph
226   generation based on the DFM paradigm. Below, we outline how DeFoG achieves these desired
227   properties for graphs.
228   Denoising Neural Architecture The denoising of DFM requires a noisy graph Gt as in-
229                                                                           θ,(n)
      put and predicts the clean marginal probability for each node x(n) via p1|t (·|Gt ) ∈ ∆X−1
230                                       θ,(ij)
231
      and for each edge e(ij) via p1|t             (·|Gt ) ∈ ∆E−1 .         They are gathered in pθ1|t (·|Gt ) =
232   (pθ,1:n:N
        1|t      (·|Gt ), pθ,1:i<j:N
                           1|t       (·|Gt )). In practice, the model receives a noisy graph where node
233   and edge features are one-hot encoded representations of their states and returns a graph of the same
234   dimensionality, with features corresponding to the node and edge marginal distributions at t = 1.
235   This formulation boils down the graph generative task to a simple graph-to-graph mapping. There-
236   fore, it is crucial to ensure maximal expressivity while preserving node permutation equivariance,
237   making a Graph Transformer (fθ ) a suitable choice for this mapping (Vignac et al., 2022).
238   Enhancing Model Expressivity Graph neural networks suffer from inherent expressivity
239   constraints (Xu et al., 2019). A usual approach to overcome their limited representation power
240   consists of explicitly augmenting the inputs with features that the networks would otherwise
241   struggle to learn. We adopt Relative Random Walk Probabilities (RRWP) encodings that are
242   proved to be expressive for both discriminative (Ma et al., 2023) and generative settings (Siraudin
243   et al., 2024). RRWP encodes the likelihood of traversing from one node to another in a graph
244
      through random walks of varying lengths. In particular, given a graph with an adjacency matrix
       A, we generate       K − 1  powers of its degree-normalized adjacency matrix, M = D−1 A, i.e.,
245
        I, M, M 2 , . . . , M K−1 . We concatenate the diagonal entries of each power to their corresponding
246
      node embedding, while combining and appending the non-diagonal to their corresponding edge
247   embeddings. RRWP features also stand out for being efficient to compute compared to the spectral
248   and cycle features used in Vignac et al. (2022), as demonstrated in Appendix G.3.
249
250
      Loss Function The nodes and edges of graphs encode distinct structural information, justifying
      their differential treatment in a graph-specific generative model. This distinction should be reflected
251
      in the training loss function. We define LDeFoG = Et∼U [0,1],p1 (G1 ),pt|1 (Gt |G1 ) CEλ (G1 , pθ1|t (·|Gt ))
252
      similarly to LDFM , in Eq. (5), with CEλ defined as follows:
253                                        X                             X
                                                     θ,(n) (n)                        θ,(ij) (ij)
254           CEλ (G1 , pθ1|t (·|Gt )) = −    log p1|t (x1 |Gt ) − λ         log p1|t (e1 |Gt ) ,              (6)
255                                        n                                     i<j
                     +
256   where λ ∈ R adjusts the weighting of nodes and edge. Empirically, setting λ > 1 improves
257   generative performance by emphasizing edges to better capture underlying node interactions.
258
      The resulting training and sampling processes are detailed in Algs. 1 and 2.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270
      Node Permutation Equivariance         We now expose DeFoG’s equivariance properties in Lemma 1.
271   (See Appendix D.2.2 for proof.)
272
      Lemma 1 (Node Permutation Equivariance and Invariance Properties of DeFoG). The DeFoG model
273
      is permutation equivariant, its loss function is permutation invariant, and its sampling probability
274
      is permutation invariant.
275
276
      4.2     E XPLORING D E F O G’ S D ESIGN S PACE
277
278   While discrete diffusion models offer a broader design space compared to one-shot models,
279   including noise schedules and diffusion trajectories (Austin et al., 2021), its exploration is costly.
280   In contrast, flow models enable greater flexibility and efficiency due to their decoupled noising
281   and sampling procedures. For instance, the number of sampling steps and their sizes are not fixed
282   as in discrete-time diffusion (Vignac et al., 2022), and the rate matrix design can be adjusted
283   independently, unlike continuous-time diffusion models (Siraudin et al., 2024; Xu et al., 2024).
284   In this section, we exploit this rich design space and propose key algorithmic improvements to
285   optimize both the training and sampling stages of DeFoG.
286
287   4.2.1     T RAINING S TAGE O PTIMIZATION
288
      Initial Distribution An essential aspect to consider is the choice of the initial distribution. This
289   distribution cannot be arbitrary. To ensure that DeFoG’s sampling probabilities are permutation
290   invariant (Lemma 1), the initial distributionQmust Q generate all permutations of a graph with equal
291   probability. A suitable distribution is pϵ = n pX ϵ
                                                                  E          X      E
                                                             i<j pϵ , where pϵ and pϵ represent shared dis-
292   tributions across nodes and edges, respectively. Campbell et al. (2024) propose two distributions that
293   fit such model: uniform, where all states have equal mass, and masked, where an additional virtual
294   state, “mask”, accumulates all mass at t = 0. We also explore additional initial distributions, includ-
295   ing the marginal distribution (Vignac et al., 2022), which represents the dataset’s marginal distribu-
296   tion, and the absorbing distribution, which places all mass at the most prevalent state in the dataset.
297   Empirically, the marginal distribution outperforms the others with a single exception, as detailed
298   further in Appendix C.1. This is supported by two key observations: first, Vignac et al. (2022)
299   demonstrate that, for any given distribution, the closest distribution within the aforementioned class
300   of factorizable initial distributions is the marginal one, thus illustrating its optimality as a prior.
301   Second, the marginal initial distribution preserves the dataset’s marginal properties throughout the
302   noising process, maintaining graph-theoretical characteristics like sparsity (Qin et al., 2023). We
303   conjecture that this fact facilitates the denoising task for the graph transformer.
304
      Train Distortion From Alg. 1, line 4, vanilla DeFoG samples t from a uniform distribution.
305
      However, in the same vein as adjusting the noise schedule in diffusion models, we can modify
306
      this distribution to allow the model to refine its predictions differently across various time regions.
307   We implement this procedure by sampling t from a uniform distribution and applying a distortion
308   function f such that t′ = f (t). The specific distortions used and their corresponding distributions
309   for t′ , are detailed in Appendix B.1. In contrast to prior findings in image generation, which suggest
310   that focusing on intermediate time regions is preferable (Esser et al., 2024), we observe that for
311   most graph generation tasks, the best performing distortion functions particularly emphasize t
312   approaching 1. Our key insight is that, as t approaches 1, discrete structures undergo abrupt tran-
313   sitions between states — from 0 to 1 in a one-hot encoding — rather than the smooth, continuous
314   refinements seen in continuous domains. Therefore, later time steps are critical for detecting errors,
315
      such as edges breaking planarity or atoms violating molecular valency rules.
316
317
      4.2.2     S AMPLING S TAGE O PTIMIZATION
318   Sample Distortion In DeFoG’s vanilla sampling process, the discretization is performed using
319   equally sized time steps (Alg. 2, line 5). Instead, we propose employing variable step sizes. This
320   adjustment is motivated by the need for more fine-grained control during certain time intervals,
321   for instance, to ensure that cluster structures are properly formed before focusing on intra-cluster
322   refinements, or to prevent edge alterations that could compromise global properties once the overall
323   structure is established. By allocating smaller, more frequent steps to these critical intervals, the
      generated graph can better capture the true properties of the data. To achieve this, we modify


                                                        6
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


324
      the evenly spaced step sizes using distortion functions (see Appendix B.1). Although the optimal
325   distortion function is highly dataset-dependent, in Appendix C.2 we propose a method to guide the
326   selection of the distortion function based on the observed training dynamics. Notably, in diffusion
327   models, the design for diffusion steps such as noise schedule is typically identical for training and
328   sampling. However, in flow models, the time distribution for training detailed in Sec. 4.2.1 and the
329   time steps used for sampling can be more flexibly disentangled. In practice, applying distortion
330   only during sampling already yields notable performance improvements.
331
      Target Guidance To ensure robust denoising performance, diffusion and flow matching mod-
332   els are designed to predict the clean data directly and subsequently generate the reverse trajectory
333   based on that prediction (Ho et al., 2020; Lipman et al.; Vignac et al., 2022). Inspired by that, we
334   propose an alternative sampling mechanism by modifying the rate matrices to Rt (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) =
335   Rt∗ (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) + Rtω (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) for zt ̸= zt+dt . , such that:
336                                                                     δ(zt+dt , z1 )
337                                        Rtω (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) = ω >0                ,                (7)
                                                                     Zt pt|1 (zt |z1 )
338
339   with ω ∈ R+ . This adjustment biases the transitions toward the clean data state z1 . Lemma 10, in
340   Appendix B.2, demonstrates that this modification introduces an O(ω) violation of the Kolmogorov
341
      equation. Consequently, choosing a small value of ω is experimentally shown to be highly benefi-
      cial, while a larger ωrestricts the distribution to regions of high probability, increasing the distance
342
      between the generated data and the training data, as indicated in Appendix B.4, Figure 8.
343
344   Stochasticity The space of valid rate matrices, i.e., those that satisfy the Kolmogorov equa-
345   tion, is not exhausted by the original formulation of Rt∗ (zt , zt+dt |z1 ). Campbell et al. (2024)
346   investigate this and show that for any rate matrix RtDB that satisfies the detailed balance condi-
347   tion, pt|1 (zt |z1 )RtDB (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) = pt|1 (zt+dt |z1 )RtDB (zt+dt , zt |z1 ), the modified rate matrix
348   Rtη = Rt∗ + ηRtDB , with η ∈ R+ , also satisfies the Kolmogorov equation. Increasing η introduces
349
      more stochasticity into the trajectory of the denoising process, while different designs of RtDB en-
      code different priors for preferred transitions between states. This mechanism can be interpreted as
350
      a correction mechanism, as it enables transitions back to states that would otherwise be disallowed
351
      according to the rate matrix formulation, as described in Appendix B.5. The effect of η across
352   different datasets is illustrated in detail in Figure 9, Appendix B.4. To further improve the sampling
353   performance of DeFoG, we also investigate the different formulations of RDB under the detailed
354   balance condition. Additional details and discussions are provided in Appendix B.3.
355
356
      Exact Expectation for Rate  h   Matrix Computationi     The execution of line 8 in Alg. 2 requires com-
                                    (d) (d) (d)     (d)                                                 (d)
357   puting Epθ,(d) (z(d) |z1:D ) Rt (zt , zt+∆t |z1 ) . In practice, Campbell et al. (2024) sample z1 ∼
                 1|t    1    t
358    θ,(d)   (d)                                                                   (d)   (d)    (d)     (d)
      p1|t (z1 |zt1:D ) to directly approximate the expectation with Rt (zt , zt+∆t |z1 ). Although this
359
      procedure converges in expectation to the intended value, it introduces more stochasticity into the
360
      denoising trajectory compared to computing the exact expectation. Given that the cardinalities of
361
      each dimension are relatively small (X and E), we explore the exact computation of the expectation.
362   This approach is especially useful in settings where precision is prioritized over diversity, ensuring
363   high confidence in the validity of generated samples, even at the expense of reduced variability.

367   In this section, we present novel theoretical results on general multivariate data, which are naturally
368   extendable to our graph-based framework, as introduced in Sec. 4.1. Their complete versions and
369   proofs are available in Appendix D.1. We begin by presenting a theoretical result that further
370   justifies the design choice for the loss function of DFM, and thus of DeFoG.

      derived upper bound with respect to θ shown in the right-hand side (RHS) of Eq. (8) corresponds

      directly to minimizing the DFM loss in Eq. (5). This further reinforces the pertinence of using such
395
      loss function, beyond its ELBO maximization motivation as proposed by Campbell et al. (2024).
396
397   Next, we demonstrate that the distributional deviation caused by the independent-dimensional Euler
398   step remains bounded, validating its utilization.
399   Theorem 3 (Simplified - Bounded deviation of the generated distribution). Let p1 be the marginal
400   distribution at t = 1 of a groundtruth CTMC, {zt1:D }0≤t≤1 , and p̃1 be the marginal distribution at
401   t = 1 of its independent-dimensional Euler sampling approximation, with a maximum step size ∆t.
402   Then, the following total variation (TV) bound holds:
403                                     ∥p1 − p̃1 ∥TV ≤ U ZD + B(ZD)2 ∆t + O(∆t),                                                        (9)
404
405   with U and B representing constant upper bounds for the right-hand side of Eq. (8) and for the
406
      denoising process relative to its noising counterpart, respectively, for any t ∈ [0, 1].
407   This result parallels Campbell et al. (2022), who established a similar bound for continuous time
408   discrete diffusion models using τ -leaping for sampling. However, Theorem 3 extends that result to
409   the DFM framework, employing the independent-dimensional Euler method. Specifically, the first
410   term of the upper bound results from the estimation error, i.e., using the neural network approxima-
            θ,(d) (d)                             (d) (d)
411   tion p1|t (z1 |zt1:D ) instead of the true p1|t (z1 |zt1:D ). As seen from Theorem 2, this term can be
412   bounded. The remaining terms arise from the time discretization and approximated simulation of
413   the CTMC, respectively. Since these terms are O(∆t), their impact can be controlled by arbitrarily
414   reducing the step size, ensuring that the generated distribution remains faithful to the ground truth.

416   6     E XPERIMENTS
417
418   First, we present DeFoG’s performance in generating graphs with diverse topological structures and
419   molecular datasets with rich prior information. A systematic evaluation of the proposed training
420
      and sampling algorithms is provided later to demonstrate the efficiency gains. Finally, we extend
      its efficacy to a real-world application in digital pathology through conditional generation. In each
421
      setting, we highlight the best result, underline the second-best, and report DeFoG’s performance
422
      with a reduced number of steps to emphasize its sampling efficiency.
423

      report the proportion of generated graphs that are valid, unique, and novel (V.U.N.), as well as the
      average ratio of distances between graph statistics of the generated and test sets relative to the train
429
      and test sets (Ratio) to assess sample quality.

      metrics. On the Tree dataset, it is only surpassed by HSpectre, which leverages a local expansion

      6.2      M OLECULAR G RAPH G ENERATION
446
447   Molecular design is a prominent real-world application of graph generation. We evaluate DeFoG’s
448   performance on this task using the QM9 (Wu et al., 2018), MOSES (Polykovskiy et al., 2020),
449   and Guacamol (Brown et al., 2019) datasets. For QM9, we follow the dataset split and evaluation
450   metrics from Vignac et al. (2022), presenting the results in Appendix F.1.2, Tab. 8. For the larger
451   MOSES and Guacamol datasets, we adhere to the training setup and evaluation metrics established
452   by Polykovskiy et al. (2020) and Brown et al. (2019), respectively, with results in Tabs. 9 and 10.
453   As illustrated in Tab. 2, DeFoG outperforms existing diffusion models. It achieving state-of the
454   Validity while preserving high uniqueness on MOSES. On Guacamol, it consistently ranks best,
455   followed by Cometh, another work utilizing a continuous-time framework. Notably, DeFoG ap-
456   proaches the performance of existing diffusion models with only 10% of the sampling steps. This
457   result is further investigated in the following section.
458
459   6.3      E FFICIENCY I MPROVEMENT
460   Sampling Efficiency As highlighted in Tabs. 1, 2 and 8, DeFoG attains similar performance, and
461   in some cases even outperforms, several diffusion-based methods with just 5% or 10% of their sam-
462   pling steps. This efficiency is a result of DeFoG’s sampling stage flexibility, whose optimization
463   is enabled due to the flexible and disentangled training-sampling procedure within DFM. To fur-
464   ther demonstrate the advantages of sampling optimization, Figure 2a shows the cumulative effect of
465   each optimization step discussed in Sec. 4.2.2. As illustrated, starting from a vanilla DeFoG model,
466   which initially performs slightly worse than DiGress, we sequentially incorporate algorithmic im-
467
      provements, with each stage indicated by an increasing number of + symbols. Each addition leads
      to a progressive improvement over the previous stage across both the Planar and QM9 datasets un-
468
      der various numbers of steps for sampling, with the final performance significantly surpassing the
469
      vanilla model using only 50 steps on the Planar dataset. While the benefits of each optimization
470   component may vary across datasets, the sampling optimization pipeline remains highly efficient,
471   enabling quick hyperparameter exploration without requiring retraining, which is a significantly
472   more resource-intensive stage (see Appendix F.2). We provide more details on the impact of each
473   sampling optimization strategy across datasets in Appendix B.4.
474
475   Training Efficiency While our primary focus is on optimizing the sampling process given its
476   computational efficiency, we emphasize that DeFoG’s training optimization strategies can further
477
      push its performance. To illustrate this, Figure 2b shows the convergence curves for the tree and
      MOSES datasets. We first observe that, with the same model, leveraging only sampling distortion
478
      can further enhance its performance beyond the vanilla implementation. This suggests that optimiz-
479
      ing the sampling procedure can be particularly useful in settings where computational resources are
480   limited and the model is undertrained, as detailed in Appendix B.6. Moreover, when an appropriate
481   sample distortion is known (e.g., polydec distortion is shown to be particularly preferred across
482   molecular datasets), applying it to both train distortion and sample distortion typically further
483   improves performance. Although DeFoG achieves faster convergence with both distortions, we
484   remark that DiGress also shows good convergence speed on the Tree dataset, due to the well-tuned
485   noise schedule employed for joint training and sampling. The mutual impact of training and sam-
      pling distortion is discussed in Appendix C.2. Besides, in the same section, we discuss a heuristic

      6.4                      C ONDITIONAL G ENERATION
509   Setup Tertiary Lymphoid Structure (TLS) graph datasets have been recently released with graphs
510   built from digital pathology data (Madeira et al., 2024). These graphs are split into two subsets —
511   low TLS and high TLS — based on their TLS content, a biologically informed metric reflecting the
512   cell organization in the graph structure. Previously, models were trained and evaluated separately for
513   each subset. To demonstrate the flexibility of DeFoG, we conditionally train it across both datasets
514
      simultaneously, using the low/high TLS content as a binary label for each graph. More details on
      the used conditional framework in Appendix E.

529
530
531   7                   C ONCLUSION
532
      We introduce DeFoG, a flexible discrete flow-matching framework for graph generation. Our
533
      theoretical contributions ensure that the denoising process preserves the graph distribution. Exten-
534   sive experiments show that DeFoG achieves state-of-the-art results across tasks such as synthetic,
535   molecular, and TLS graph generation, demonstrating real-world applicability and efficiency
536   with just 50 sampling steps. Although limited computational resources restricted a thorough
537   hyperparameter search, leaving room for further optimization, DeFoG’s performance highlights its
538   potential. While our work focuses on graphs, the flexibility of DeFoG and the proposed theoretical
539   guarantees suggest that some of our techniques may generalize to other data modalities, opening
      interesting avenues for future research.

541
542
      All authors of this paper have read and adhered to the ICLR Code of Ethics. We explicitly acknowl-
543   edge our commitment to these ethical guidelines throughout the submission process.
544
545   Regarding impact, the primary objective of this paper is to advance graph generation under a more
546
      flexible framework, with applications spanning general graph generation, molecular design, and
      digital pathology. The ability to generate graphs with discrete labels can have broad-reaching im-
547
      plications for fields such as drug discovery and diagnostic technologies. While this development
548
      has the potential to bring about both positive and negative societal or ethical impacts, particularly
549   in areas like biomedical and chemical research, we currently do not foresee any immediate societal
550   concerns associated with the proposed methodology.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


918
      A     C ONTEXTUALIZING R ELATED R ESEARCH
919
920   In this section, we further contextualize DeFoG within the scope of related work. We begin by
921   introducing the methods used for comparison with DeFoG in Appendix A.1. Subsequently, we
922   outline the key distinctions between DeFoG and existing diffusion-based graph generative models
923   in Appendix A.2.
924
925   A.1     OVERVIEW OF C OMPARED M ETHODS
926
927   In Sec. 6, we evaluate DeFoG against a diverse set of graph generative models, which we introduce
928   below:
929           • GraphRNN (You et al., 2018) and GRAN (Liao et al., 2019), two pioneering autoregressive
930             models for graph generation;
931           • SPECTRE (Martinkus et al., 2022), a spectrally conditioned GAN-based model for graph
932             generation;
933           • DiGress (Vignac et al., 2022), the first discrete diffusion model for graph generation;
934           • EDGE (Chen et al., 2023), a discrete diffusion model leveraging graph sparsity and degree
935
                guidance for scalability.
              • BwR (Diamant et al., 2023), which focuses on efficient graph representations via bandwidth
936
                restriction schemes that are compatible with various graph generation models. We report
937
                its results in combination with EDP-GNN (Niu et al., 2020), which was the first graph
938             diffusion model;
939           • BiGG (Dai et al., 2020), an autoregressive model that exploits graph sparsity and training
940             parallelization to scale to larger graphs;
941           • GraphGen (Goyal et al., 2020), a scalable autoregressive approach utilizing graph can-
942             onization with minimum DFS codes, notable for being domain-agnostic and inherently
943             supporting attributed graphs;
944           • HSpectre (Bergmeister et al., 2023), a hierarchical graph generation method that utilizes a
945             score-based formulation for iterative local expansion steps;
946
              • DisCo (Xu et al., 2024) and Cometh (Siraudin et al., 2024), two continuous-time discrete
                diffusion models for graph generation;
947
              • GruM (Jo et al., 2024), which employs a diffusion mixture to explicitly learn the final graph
948
                topology and structure;
949           • CatFlow (Eijkelboom et al., 2024), which results from the instantiation of variational flow
950             matching to graph generation.
951
952   A.2     D E F O G AND G RAPH D IFFUSION M ODELS
953
954   In this section, we contextualize DeFoG in relation to existing graph diffusion models.
955
956   A.2.1    F ROM C ONTINUOUS TO D ISCRETE S TATE - SPACES
957
      Early diffusion-based graph generative models extended continuous diffusion and score-based
958
      methods from image generation to graphs by relaxing adjacency matrices into continuous state-
959
      spaces (Niu et al., 2020; Jo et al., 2022). However, this approach overlooks the inherent discreteness
960   of graph-structured data, resulting in topologically uninformed noising processes. For instance,
961   these methods often destroy graph sparsity and generate noisy complete graphs (Vignac et al., 2022;
962   Xu et al., 2024; Siraudin et al., 2024), making it more challenging for denoising neural networks to
963   recover meaningful structural properties from the noisy inputs. Some recent formulations operating
964   on continuous state-spaces have tried to overcome these limitations: GruM (Jo et al., 2024) intro-
965   duces an endpoint-conditioned diffusion mixture strategy to enhance accuracy by explicitly learning
966   final graph structures, while CatFlow (Eijkelboom et al., 2024) proposes variational flow matching
967   to handle categorical data more effectively.
968   Alternatively, discrete diffusion models have emerged as a more natural solution, directly preserving
969   the discrete nature of graph data (Vignac et al., 2022; Haefeli et al.). These models have demon-
970   strated state-of-the-art performance across a variety of applications, including neural architecture
971   search (Asthana et al., 2024), combinatorial optimization (Sun & Yang, 2024), molecular genera-
      tion (Irwin et al., 2024), and reaction pathway design (Igashov et al., 2024).


                                                        18
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


972
       DeFoG aligns with this second family of methods, modeling nodes and edges in discrete state-spaces
973    to leverage the structural properties of graph data effectively.
974
975    A.2.2    F ROM D ISCRETE TO C ONTINUOUS TIME
976
977    The initial discrete-time diffusion frameworks for graph generation (Vignac et al., 2022; Haefeli
978    et al.) were built upon Discrete Denoising Diffusion Probabilistic Models (D3PMs) (Austin et al.,
979    2021), which operate with a fixed partitioning of time. This discretization constrains the model to
980
       denoise at specific time points and ties the sampling process to the same fixed time steps used during
       training, leading to a rigid coupling between the training and sampling stages. Such inflexibility in
981
       time discretization can limit the quality of generated graphs.
982
983    In contrast, continuous-time discrete diffusion frameworks (Campbell et al., 2022; Sun et al., 2022)
984    overcome these limitations by enabling the model to denoise at arbitrary time points within a contin-
985    uous interval (typically between 0 and 1). This flexibility allows the time discretization strategy for
986
       sampling to be selected post-training, enabling the use of advanced sampling techniques (Jolicoeur-
       Martineau et al., 2021; Zhang & Chen, 2022; Salimans & Ho, 2022; Chung et al., 2022; Song et al.,
987
       2020; Dockhorn et al., 2021) to improve generation performance. These continuous-time frame-
988
       works have been successfully extended to graph generative models (Xu et al., 2024; Siraudin et al.,
989    2024), achieving notable improvements.
990
991    DeFoG follows a continuous-time formulation, leveraging its flexibility in sampling to achieve en-
992
       hanced performance while maintaining the strengths of discrete state-space modeling.
993
994
       A.2.3    F ROM C ONTINUOUS -T IME D ISCRETE D IFFUSION TO D ISCRETE F LOW M ATCHING
995    While both continuous-time discrete diffusion and discrete flow matching (DFM) share the CTMC
996    formulation for the denoising process, they differ fundamentally in the formulation of the noising
997    process. Continuous-time discrete diffusion-based graph generative models (Xu et al., 2024; Sir-
998    audin et al., 2024) define the noising process as a CTMC, akin to the denoising process. However,
999    this approach imposes two significant limitations:
1000
1001
             1. Incomplete Coupling of Training and Sampling: The rate matrices of the noising and
                denoising processes are explicitly interrelated, and the noising rate matrix must be fixed
1002
                during training. This restricts the sampling stage, preventing full decoupling of training
1003
                and sampling.
1004
1005
             2. Limited Design Space: The noising process must be derived analytically, which is not
                straightforward and is only feasible for rate matrices suitable for matrix exponentiation.
1006
                Additionally, the denoising rate matrix is implicitly defined during training, constraining
1007
                the flexibility of the denoising trajectory at sampling time (e.g., fixing the level of stochas-
1008            ticity).
1009
1010   In contrast, DeFoG allows for direct prescription of the noising process, pt|1 , without these con-
1011   straints. The rate matrix for the denoising process is selected exclusively at sampling time, fully
1012   decoupling the training and sampling stages. This flexibility enables performance optimization dur-
1013   ing sampling, such as tuning the stochasticity of the denoising trajectory via RtDB or adjusting target
1014   guidance magnitude with Rtω .
1015   The benefits of this decoupled framework are evident in Figures 2, 6 and 7, which demonstrate that
1016   the vanilla DeFoG configuration alone does not outperform existing diffusion-based graph genera-
1017   tive models. However, our extensive sampling optimization pipeline capitalizes on DeFoG’s flexible
1018   design space to achieve state-of-the-art results. These observations align with findings in iterative
1019   refinement methods across other data modalities. For instance, Karras et al. (2022) elaborate on
1020   the benefits of stochasticity adjustment in denoising trajectories within diffusion models for image
1021
       generation.
1022   For a comprehensive discussion of the differences between continuous-time discrete diffusion and
1023   DFM frameworks, see Appendix H of (Campbell et al., 2024).
1024
1025



                                                          19
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1026
       B     S AMPLE O PTIMIZATION
1027
1028   In this section, we explore the proposed sampling optimization in more detail. We start by analysing
1029   the different time distortion functions in Appendix B.1. Next, in Appendix B.2, we prove that the
1030   proposed target guidance mechanism actually satisfies the Kolmogorov equation, thus yielding valid
1031   rate matrices and, in Appendix B.3, we provide more details about the detailed balance equation
1032   and how it widens the design space of rate matrices. In Appendix B.4, we also describe the adopted
1033   sampling optimization pipeline. Finally, in Appendix B.5, we provide more details to better clarify
1034   the dynamics of the sampling process.
1035
1036   B.1    T IME D ISTORTION F UNCTIONS
1037
       In Sec. 4, we explore the utilization of different distortion functions, i.e., functions that are used
1038
       to transform time. The key motivation for employing such functions arises from prior work on
1039
       flow matching in image generation, where skewing the time distribution during training has been
1040   shown to significantly enhance empirical performance (Esser et al., 2024). In practical terms, this
1041   implies that the model is more frequently exposed to specific time intervals. Mathematically, this
1042   transformation corresponds to introducing a time-dependent re-weighting factor in the loss function,
1043   biasing the model to achieve better performance in particular time ranges.
1044
       In our case, we apply time distortions to the probability density function (PDF) by introducing
1045
       a function f that transforms the original uniformly sampled time t, such that t′ = f (t) for t ∈
1046   [0, 1]. These time distortion functions must satisfy certain conditions: they must be monotonic, with
1047   f (0) = 0 and f (1) = 1. Although the space of functions that satisfy these criteria is infinite, we
1048   focus on five distinct functions that yield fundamentally different profiles for the PDF of t′ . Our goal
1049   is to gain intuition about which time ranges are most critical for graph generation and not to explore
1050   that function space exhaustively. Specifically:
1051
              • Polyinc: f (t) = t2 , yielding a PDF that decreases monotonically with t′ ;
1052
1053          • Cos: f (t) = 1−cos 2
                                      πt
                                         , creating a PDF with high density near the boundaries t′ = 0 and
1054
                t = 1, and low for intermediate t′ ;
                 ′

1055          • Identity: f (t) = t, resulting in a uniform PDF for t′ ∈ [0, 1];
1056          • Revcos: f (t) = 2t − 1−cos    2
                                                 πt
                                                    , leading to high PDF density for intermediate t′ and low
1057            density at the extremes t = 0 and t′ = 1;
                                            ′

1058          • Polydec: f (t) = 2t − t2 , where the PDF increases monotonically with t′ .
1059
1060   The PDF resulting from applying a monotonic function f to a random variable t is given by:
1061                                                              d
                                              ϕt′ (t′ ) = ϕt (t) ′ f −1 (t′ ) ,
1062                                                             dt
1063   where ϕt (t) and ϕt′ (t′ ) denote the PDFs of t and t′ , respectively. In our case, ϕt (t) = 1 for t ∈ [0, 1].
1064   The distortion functions and their corresponding PDFs are illustrated in Figure 3.
1065
       One of the strategies the proposed in sampling optimization procedure is the use of variable step
1066
       sizes throughout the denoising process. This is achieved by mapping evenly spaced time points
1067   (DeFoG’s vanilla version) through a transformation that follows the same constraints as the training
1068   time distortions discussed earlier. We employ the same set of time distortion functions, again not to
1069   exhaustively explore the space of applicable functions, but to gain insight into how varying step sizes
1070   affect graph generation. The expected step sizes for each distortion can be directly inferred from
1071   Figure 3. For instance, the polydec function leads to progressively smaller time steps, suggesting
1072   more refined graph edits in the denoising process as t′ approaches 1.
1073   Note that even though we apply the same time distortions for both training and sample stages, in
1074   each setting they have different objectives: in training, the time distortions skew the PDFs from
1075   where t′ is sampled, while in sampling they vary the step sizes.
1076

       B.3       D ETAILED BALANCE , P RIOR I NCORPORATION , AND S TOCHASTICITY
1207
1208   Campbell et al. (2024) show that although their z1 -conditional formulation of Rt∗ generates pt|1 , it
1209   does not span the full space of valid rate matrices — those that satisfy the conditional Kolmogorov
1210   equation (Eq. (11)). They derive sufficient conditions for identifying other valid rate matrices. No-
1211   tably, they demonstrate that matrices of the form
1212
                                                             Rtη := Rt∗ + ηRtDB ,
1213
1214   with η ∈ R≥0 and RtDB any matrix that verifies the detailed balance condition:
1215                           pt|1 (zt |z1 )RtDB (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) = pt|1 (zt+dt |z1 )RtDB (zt+dt , zt |z1 ),               (12)
1216
1217   still satisfy the Kolmogorov equation. The detailed balance condition ensures that the outflow,
1218   pt|1 (zt |z1 )RtDB (zt , zt+dt |z1 ), and inflow, pt|1 (zt+dt |z1 )RtDB (zt+dt , zt |z1 ), of probability mass to any
1219
       given state are perfectly balanced. Under these conditions, this additive component’s contribution to
       the Kolmogorov equation becomes null (similar to the target guidance, as shown in the proof of of
1220
       Lemma 10, in Appendix B.2).
1221
1222   A natural question is how to choose a suitable design for RtDB from the infinite space of detailed
1223   balance rate matrices. As depicted in Figure 4, this flexibility can be leveraged to incorporate pri-
1224   ors into the denoising model by encouraging specific transitions between states. By adjusting the
1225
       sparsity of the matrix entries, additional transitions beyond those prescribed by Rt∗ can be intro-
       duced. In the general case, transitions between all states are possible; in the column case, a specific
1226
       state centralizes all potential transitions; and in the single-entry case, only transitions between two
1227
       states are permitted. These examples merely illustrate some possibilities and do not exhaust the
1228   range of potential RtDB designs. The matrix entries can be structured by considering the following
1229   reorganization of terms of Eq. (12):
1230
                                                                    pt|1 (zt |z1 )
1231                                 RtDB (zt+dt , zt |z1 ) =                       RDB (zt , zt+dt |z1 ).
1232                                                               pt|1 (zt+dt |z1 ) t
1233   Therefore, a straightforward approach is to assign the lower triangular entries of the
1234   rate matrix as RtDB (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) = pt|1 (zt+dt |z1 ), and the upper triangular entries as
1235   RtDB
1236
          P(zt+dt , zt |z1 ) = pt|1 (zt |z1 ). The diagonal entries are computed last to ensure that Rt (zt , zt ) =
       − zt+dt ̸=zt Rt (zt , zt+dt ).
1237
1238   We incorporated various types of priors into RDB by preserving specific rows or entries in the matrix.
1239   Specifically, we experimented with retaining the column corresponding to the state with the highest
1240   marginal distribution (Column - Max Marginal), the column corresponding to the predicted x1 states
1241   (Column - x1 ), and the columns corresponding to the state with the highest probability in pt|1 .
       Additionally, we tested the approach of retaining only RDB (xt , i) where i is the state with the highest

       marginal distribution (Entry - Max Marginal). For instance, under the absorbing initial distribution,
1269
       this state is the one to which all data is absorbed at t = 0. We note that there remains significant
1270   space for exploration by adjusting the weights assigned to different positions within RDB , as the only
1271   condition that must be satisfied is that symmetrical positions adhere to a specific proportionality.
1272   However, in practice, none of the specific designs illustrated in Figure 4 showed a clear advantage
1273   over others in the settings we evaluated. As a result, we chose the general case for our experiments,
1274   as it offers the most flexibility by incorporating the least prior knowledge.
1275
       Orthogonal to the design of RtDB , we must also consider the hyperparameter η, which regulates the
1276   magnitude of stochasticity in the denoising process. Specifically, setting η = 0 (thereby relying
1277   solely on Rt∗ ) minimizes the expected number of jumps throughout the denoising trajectory under
1278   certain conditions, as shown by Campbell et al. (2024) in Proposition 3.4. However, in continuous
1279   diffusion models, some level of stochasticity has been demonstrated to enhance performance (Karras
1280   et al., 2022; Cao et al., 2023; Xu et al., 2023). Conversely, excessive stochasticity can negatively
1281   impact performance. Campbell et al. (2024) propose that there exists an optimal level of stochasticity
1282   that strikes a balance between exploration and accuracy. In our experiments, we observed varied
1283   behaviors as η increases, resulting in different performance outcomes across datasets, as illustrated
1284
       in Figure 9.
1285
1286   B.4               H YPERPARAMETER O PTIMIZATION P IPELINE
1287
       A significant advantage of flow matching methods is their inherently greater flexibility in the sam-
1288
       pling process compared to diffusion models, as they are more disentangled from the training stage.
1289
       Each of the proposed optimization strategies exposed in Sec. 4.2.2 expands the search space for
1290   optimal performance. However, conducting a full grid search across all those methodologies is
1291   impractical for the computational resources available. To address this challenge, our sampling opti-
1292   mization pipeline consists of, for each of the proposed optimization strategies, all hyperparameters
1293   are held constant at their default values except for the parameter controlling the chosen strategy,
1294   over which we perform a sweep. The optimal values obtained for each strategy are combined to
1295   form the final configuration. In Tab. 6, we present the final hyperparameter values obtained for each
       dataset. This pipeline is sufficient to achieve state-of-the-art performance, which reinforces the ex-

1323
       pressivity of DeFoG. We expect to achieve even better results if a more comprehensive search of the
       hyperparameter space was carried out.
1324
1325   To better mode detailedly illustrate the influence of each sampling optimization, we show in Fig-
1326   ures 6 and 7 in present the impact of varying parameter values across the synthetic datasets Figure 6
1327   and molecular datasets in Figure 7 used in this work.
1328   While Figures 6 and 7 are highly condensed, we provide a more fine-grained version that specifically
1329   illustrates the influence of the hyperparameters η and ω. This version highlights their impact when
1330   generating with the full number of steps (500 and 1000 for molecular and synthetic data, respec-
1331   tively) and with 50 steps. As emphasized in Figures 8 and 9, the influence of these hyperparameters
1332   varies across datasets and exhibits distinct behaviors depending on the number of steps used.
1333   Several key observations can be made here. First, since the stochasticity is designed around the de-
1334   tailed balance condition, which holds more rigorously with increased precision, it generally provides
1335   greater benefits with the full generation steps but leads to a more pronounced performance decrease
1336   when generating with only 50 steps. Additionally, for datasets such as Planar, MOSES, and Gua-
1337   camol, the stochasticity shows an increasing-then-decreasing behavior, indicating the presence of an
1338   optimal value. Furthermore, while target guidance significantly improves validity across different
1339   datasets, it can negatively affect novelty and the average ratio when set too high. This suggests that
1340   excessive target guidance may promote overfitting to high-probability regions of the training set,
1341
       distorting the overall distribution. In conclusion, each hyperparameter should be carefully chosen
       based on the specific objective.
1342
1343   To demonstrate the benefit of each designed optimization step, we report the step-wise improvements
1344   by sequentially adding each tuned step across the primary datasets—synthetic datasets in Figure 10
1345   and molecular datasets in Figure 11—used in this work.
1346
1347   B.5                        U NDERSTANDING THE S AMPLING DYNAMICS OF Rt∗

1349   In this section, we aim to provide deeper intuition into the sampling dynamics imposed by the design
       of Rt∗ , as proposed by Campbell et al. (2024). The explicit formulation of Rt∗ can be found in Eq. (3).

1379
1380
1381
       Notably, the denominator in the expression serves as a normalizing factor, meaning the dynamics of
1382
       each sampling step are primarily influenced by the values in the numerator. Specifically, we observe
1383   the following relationship:
1384
1385
                                                                    ∂t pt|1 (zt |z1 ) = δ(zt , z1 ) + p0 (zt ),
1386
       derived by directly differentiating Eq. (1). Based on this, the possible values of Rt∗ for different
1387   combinations of zt and zt+dt are outlined in Tab. 4.
1388
                                 Table 4: Values of Rt∗ for different zt and zt+dt .
1389
1390                           C ONDITION               R∗ (xt , j|z1 )                                                      I NTUITION
1391                           zt = z1 , zt+dt = z1     ReLU(p0 (z1 ) − p0 (z1 )) = 0                                        N O TRANSITION
1392                           zt = z1 , zt+dt ̸= z1    ReLU(p0 (zt ) − 1 − p0 (zt+dt )) = 0                                 N O TRANSITION
1393                           zt ̸= z1 , zt+dt = z1    ReLU(p0 (zt ) − p0 (zt+dt ) + 1) > 0                                 T RANSITION TO z1
                               zt ̸= z1 , zt+dt ̸= z1   ReLU(p0 (zt ) − p0 (zt+dt ))                                         T RANSITION TO zt+dt I F p0 (zt ) > p0 (zt+dt )
1394
1395   From the first two lines of Tab. 4, we observe that once the system reaches the predicted state z1 , it
1396   remains there. If not, Rt∗ only encourages transitions to other states under two conditions: either the
1397   target state is z1 (third line), or the corresponding entries in the initial distribution for potential next
1398   states have smaller values than the current state (fourth line). As a result, the sampling dynamics are
1399   heavily influenced by the initial distribution, as discussed further in Appendix C.1.
1400   For instance, with the masking distribution, the fourth line facilitates transitions to states other than
1401   the virtual “mask” state, whereas for the uniform distribution, no transitions are allowed. For the
1402   marginal distribution, transitions are directed toward less likely states. Note that while these behav-
1403   iors hold when the rate matrix consists solely of Rt∗ , additional transitions can be introduced through
       RtDB (as detailed in Appendix B.3) or by applying target guidance (see Appendix B.2).

       B.6                P ERFORMANCE I MPROVEMENT FOR U NDERTRAINED M ODELS
1442
1443   In this section, we present the performance of a model trained on the QM9 dataset and the Planar
1444   dataset using only 30% of the epochs compared to the final model being reported. We employ the
1445   same hyperparameters with Tab. 8 and Tab. 1 for the sampling setup, as reported in Tab. 6.
1446   Compared to fully trained models, our model achieves 99.0 validity (vs. 99.3) and 96.4 uniqueness
1447   (vs. 96.3) on the QM9 dataset. For the Planar dataset, it attains 95.5 validity (vs. 99.5) and an
1448   average ratio of 1.4 (vs. 1.6). These results demonstrate that, even with significantly fewer training
1449   epochs, the model maintains competitive performance under a well-designed sampling procedure,
1450   although extended training can still further improve performance. Notably, all metrics surpass the
1451   discrete-time diffusion benchmark DiGress (Vignac et al., 2022). As a result, the optimization in the
1452   sampling stage proves particularly beneficial when computational resources are limited, by enhanc-
1453   ing the performance of an undertrained model.

1574
1575
       C                          T RAIN O PTIMIZATION
1576
1577
       In this section, we provide a more detailed analysis of the influence of the various training optimiza-
1578
       tion strategies introduced in Sec. 4.2.1. In Appendix C.1, we empirically demonstrate the impact
1579   of selecting different initial distributions on performance, while in Appendix C.2, we examine the
1580   interaction between training and sampling optimization.
1581
1582
       C.1                        I NITIAL D ISTRIBUTIONS
1583
1584   Under DeFoG’s framework, the noising process for each dimension is modeled as a linear interpo-
1585   lation between the clean data distribution (the one-hot representation of the current state) and an
1586   initial distribution, p0 . As such, it is intuitive that different initial distributions result in varying
1587   performances, depending on the denoising dynamics they induce. In particular, they have a direct
1588   impact on the sampling dynamics through Rt∗ (see Appendix B.5) and may also pose tasks of varying
1589   difficulty for the graph transformer. In this paper, we explore four distinct initial distributions2 :

1590   Uniform: p0 = Z1 , Z1 , . . . , Z1 ∈ ∆Z−1 . Here, the probability mass is uniformly distributed across
1591   all states, as proposed by Campbell et al. (2024).
1592
       Masking: p0 = [0, 0, . . . , 0, 1] ∈ ∆Z . In this setting, all the probability mass collapses into a new
1593
       “mask” state at t = 0, as introduced by Campbell et al. (2024).
1594
1595   Marginal: p0 = [m1 , m2 , . . . , mZ ] ∈ ∆Z−1 , where mi denotes the marginal probability of the
1596   i-th state in the dataset. This approach is widely used in state-of-the-art graph generation models
1597
       (Vignac et al., 2022; Xu et al., 2024; Siraudin et al., 2024).
1598   Absorbing: p0 = [0, . . . , 1, . . . , 0] ∈ ∆Z−1 , representing a one-hot encoding of the most common
1599   state (akin to applying an argmax operator to the marginal initial distribution).
1600
       In Figure 12, we present the training curves for each initial distribution for three different datasets.
1601
1602   We observe that the marginal distribution consistently achieves at least as good performance as the
1603   other initial distributions. This, along with the theoretical reasons outlined in Sec. 4.2.1, reinforces
1604
       its use as the default initial distribution for DeFoG. The only dataset where marginal was surpassed
       was the SBM dataset, which we attribute to its inherently different nature (stochastic vs. determin-
1605
       istic). In this case, the absorbing distribution emerged as the best-performing choice. Interestingly,
1606
       the absorbing distribution also tends to converge faster across datasets.
1607
1608   Lastly, it is worth noting that in discrete diffusion models for graphs, predicting the best limit noise
1609   distribution based solely on dataset characteristics remains, to our knowledge, an open question
1610
       (Tseng et al., 2023). We expect this complexity to extend to discrete flow models as well. Although
       this is outside the scope of our work, we view this as an exciting direction for future research.
1611
1612
1613
       C.2                        I NTERACTION BETWEEN S AMPLE AND T RAIN D ISTORTIONS
1614
       From Appendix B.4, we observe that time distortions applied during the sampling stage can sig-
1615   nificantly affect performance. This suggests that graph discrete flow models do not behave evenly
1616   across time and are more sensitive to specific time intervals, where generative performance benefits
1617   from finer updates achieved by using smaller time steps. Building on this observation, we extended
1618   our analysis to the training stage, exploring two main questions:
1619
                            2
                                Recall that Z represents the cardinality of the state space, and ∆Z−1 the associated probability simplex.

1646
1647                                                              Figure 13: Interaction between training and sampling time distortions.
1648
1649
1650                                                  • Is there an universally optimal training time distortion for graph generation across different
1651                                                    datasets?
1652                                                  • How do training and sampling time distortions interact? Is there alignment between the
1653                                                    two? Specifically, if we understand the effect of a time distortion at one stage (training or
1654                                                    sampling), can we infer its impact at the other?
1655
1656   To investigate these questions, we conducted a grid search. For two datasets, we trained five models,
1657   each with a different time distortion applied during training. Subsequently, we tested each model by
1658
       applying the five different distortions at the sampling stage. The results are presented in Figure 13.
1659   The results vary by dataset. For QM9, validity appears primarily influenced by the sampling distor-
1660   tion method, with a preference for distortions that encourage smaller steps at the end of the denoising
1661   process (such as polydec and cos). However, for FCD3 , the training distortion plays a more signifi-
1662   cant role.
1663   For the planar dataset, we observe a near-perfect alignment between training and sampling dis-
1664   tortions in terms of validity, with a clear preference for more accurate training models and finer
1665   sampling predictions closer to t = 1. The results for the average ratio metric, however, are less
1666   consistent and show volatility.
1667
       These findings help address our core questions: The interaction between training and sampling
1668   distortions, as well as the best training time distortion, is dataset-dependent. Nonetheless, for the
1669   particular case of the planar dataset, we observe a notable alignment between training and sampling
1670   distortions. This alignment suggests that times close to t = 1 are critical for correctly generat-
1671   ing planar graphs. We conjecture that this alignment can be attributed to planarity being a global
1672   property that arises from local constraints, as captured by Kuratowski’s Theorem, which states that
1673
               3
                            FCD is calculated only for valid molecules, so this metric may inherently reflect survival bias.
