1682
1683   Figure 14: In the left figure, we present the cross-entropy (CE) loss used for training at different
1684   time steps across various stages of the training process. The right figure shows the ratio of each CE
1685   loss trajectory relative to the last one, illustrating the overall training trend and emphasizing which
1686   parts of the model are predominantly learned over time.
1687
1688
1689
       a graph is non-planar if and only if it contains a subgraph reducible to K5 or K3,3 through edge
       contraction (Kuratowski, 1930).
1690
1691
       Loss Tracker To determine if the structural properties observed in datasets like the planar dataset
1692
       can be detected and exploited without requiring an exhaustive sweep over all possibilities, we pro-
1693   pose developing a metric that quantifies the difficulty of predicting the clean graph for any given
1694   time point t ∈ [0, 1). For this, we perform a sweep over t for a given model, where for each t, we
1695   noise a sufficiently large batch of clean graphs and evaluate the model’s training loss on them. This
1696   yields a curve that shows how the training loss varies as a function of t. We then track how this
1697   curve evolves across epochs. To make the changes more explicit, we compute the ratio of the loss
1698   curve relative to the fully trained model’s values. These curves are shown in Figure 14.
1699   As expected, the curve of training loss as a function of t (left in Figure 14) is monotonically decreas-
1700   ing, indicating that as graphs are decreasingly noised, the task becomes simpler. However, the most
1701   interesting insights arise from the evolution of this curve across epochs (right in Figure 14). We
1702   observe that for smaller values of t, the model reaches its maximum capacity early in the training
1703   process, showing no significant improvements after the initial few epochs. In contrast, for larger
1704   values of t (closer to t = 1), the model exhibits substantial improvements throughout the training
1705   period. This suggests that the model can continue to refine its predictions in the time range where the
1706   task is easier. These findings align with those in Figure 13, reinforcing our expectation that training
1707
       the model to be more precise in this range or providing more refined sampling steps will naturally
       enhance performance in the planar dataset.
1708
1709   These insights offer a valuable understanding of the specific dynamics at play within the planar
1710   dataset. Nevertheless, the unique structural characteristics of each dataset may influence the interac-
1711   tion between training and sampling time distortions in ways that are not captured here. Future work
1712   could explore these dynamics across a wider range of datasets to assess the generalizability of our
1713
       findings.

       D     T HEORETICAL R ESULTS
1729
1730   In this section, we provide the proofs of the different theoretical results of the paper. We first provide
1731   the results that are domain agnostic, i.e., that hold for any data modality that lies in discrete state-
1732   spaces, and then the graph specific ones.
1733
1734   D.1     D OMAIN AGNOSTIC T HEORETICAL R ESULTS
1735
1736   Here, we first provide the proof of Theorem 2 in Appendix D.1.1, and then the proof of Theorem 3
1737   in Appendix D.1.2.
1738
1739   D.1.1     B OUNDED E STIMATION E RROR OF U NCONDITIONAL M ULTIVARIATE R ATE M ATRIX
1740   We start by introducing two important concepts, which will reveal important for the proof of the
1741   intended result.
1742
1743   Unconditional Multivariate Rate Matrix As exposed in Sec. 3.1, the marginal distribution and
1744   the rate matrix of a CTMC are related by the Kolmogorov equation:
1745                  ∂t pt = RtT pt
1746                            X                                  X
1747                        =        Rt (zt+dt , zt )pt (zt+dt ) −   Rt (zt , zt+dt )pt (zt ) .

1749                                          Probability Inflow                          Probability Outflow
1750   The expansion in the second equality reveals the conservation law inherent in the Kolmogorov equa-
1751   tion, illustrating that the time derivative of the marginal distribution represents the net balance be-
1752   tween the inflow and outflow of probability mass at a given state.
1753
       Importantly, in the multivariate case, the (joint) rate matrix can be expressed through the following
1754
       decomposition:

1769   tributions. While it can be defined more generally, this paper focuses on its application to discrete
1770   probability distributions over a finite sample space Z. In particular, for two discrete probability
1771   distributions P and Q, their total variation distance is defined as:

       D.1.2      B OUNDED D EVIATION OF THE G ENERATED D ISTRIBUTION
1891
1892   As proceeded in Appendix D.1.1, we start by introducing the necessary concepts that will reveal
1893   useful for the proof of the intended result.
1894
1895   On the Choice of the CTMC Sampling Method                    Generating new samples using DFM amounts
1896   to simulate a multivariate CTMC according to:
1897                                    1:D
                              pt+dt|t (zt+dt |zt1:D ) = δ(zt1:D , zt+dt
                                                                   1:D
                                                                        ) + Rt (zt1:D , zt+dt
                                                                                         1:D
                                                                                              )dt,             (18)
1898
1899   where Rt (zt1:D , zt+dt
                          1:D
                               ) denotes the unconditional multivariate rate matrix defined in Eq. (13). This
1900   process can be simulated exactly using Gillespie’s Algorithm (Gillespie, 1976; 1977). However,
1901   such an algorithm does not scale for large D (Campbell et al., 2022). Although τ -leaping is a
1902   widely adopted approximate algorithm to address this limitation (Gillespie, 2001), it requires ordinal
1903
       discrete state spaces, which is suitable for cases like text or images but not for graphs. Therefore, we
       cannot apply it in the context of this paper. Additionally, directly replacing the infinitesimal step dt
1904
       in Eq. (13) with a finite time step ∆t à la Euler method is inappropriate, as Rt (zt1:D , zt+dt
                                                                                                   1:D
                                                                                                        ) prevents
1905
       state transitions in more than one dimension per step under the continuous framework. Instead,
1906   Campbell et al. (2024) propose an approximation where the Euler step is applied independently to
1907   each dimension, as seen in Eq. (4).
1908
1909
       In this section, we theoretically demonstrate that, despite its approximation, the independent-
       dimensional Euler sampling method error remains bounded and can be made arbitrarily small by
1910
       reducing the step size ∆t or by reducing the estimation error of the rate matrix.
1911
1912
       Markov Kernel of a CTMC For this proof, we also introduce the notion of Markov kernel of
1913   a CTMC. As previously seen, the rate matrix (or generator), Rt , characterizes the infinitesimal
1914   transition rates between states, governing the dynamics of the process. The Markov kernel, Rt , is
1915   a function that provides the transition probabilities between states over a finite time interval. For
1916   example, for a univariate CTMC with a state space Z, the Markov kernel Rt (s, t) is a matrix where
1917   each entry Rt, ij (s, s′ ) represents the probability that the single variable transitions from state i
                                                   ′                                                         ′
1918   P state j during′ the time interval [s, s ]. These matrices are stochastic, i.e., for fixed s and s ,
       to
1919      j∈S  R t, ij (s, s ) = 1, ∀i. This contrasts with the rate matrix where rows sum to  1. Additionally,
1920   Markov kernels must also respect the initial condition Rt, ij (s, s) = IZ×Z 5 . Importantly, a constant
1921   rate matrix, R, between t and t + ∆t yields the following Markov kernel:
1922
                                                   R(t, t + ∆t) = eR∆t                                         (19)
1923
1924
       We are now in conditions of proceeding to the proof of Theorem 3. We start by first proving that, in
1925   the univariate case, the time derivatives of the conditional rate matrices are upper bounded.
1926
       Lemma 5 (Upper bound time derivative of conditional univariate rate matrix). For t ∈ (0, 1),
1927
       zt , zt+dt , z1 ∈ Z, with zt ̸= zt+dt , then we have:

1932   Proof. Recall that pt|1 (zt |z1 ) = t δ(zt , z1 ) + (1 − t) p0 (zt ) (from Eq. (1)). Two different cases must
1933   then be considered.
1934   In the first case, pt|1 (zt |z1 ) = 0. This implies that both extremes of the linear interpolation are
1935   0. In that case, the linear interpolation will be identically 0 for t ∈ (0, 1). Thus, by definition,
1936   Rt∗ (zt , zt+dt |z1 ) = 0 for t ∈ (0, 1), which implies that |∂t Rt∗ (zt , zt+dt |z1 )| = 0.

       Assumption 6. For zt1:D ∈ Z D , z1 ∈ Z and t ∈ [0, 1], for each variable z (d) of a joint variable
1973                                    (d)                    (d)           (d)      (d) (d)

1974
1975
       This assumption states that the denoising process is upper bounded by a quadratic term on the
1976   noising process. This assumption is reasonable because, while the noising term applies individually
1977   to each component of the data, the denoising process operates on the joint variable, allowing for
1978   a more comprehensive and interdependent correction that reflects the combined influence of all
1979   components.

2021   Theorem 3 (Bounded deviation of the generated distribution). Let {zt1:D }t∈[0,1] ∈ Z D × [0, 1] be a

2214
       D.2     G RAPH S PECIFIC T HEORETICAL R ESULTS
2215
2216   We now proceed to the graph specific theoretical results.
2217
2218   D.2.1    A DDITIONAL F EATURES E XPRESSIVITY
2219
2220   This section explains the expressivity of the RRWP features used in DeFoG. We summarize the
2221   findings of Ma et al. (2023) in Proposition 7, who establish that, by encoding random walk proba-
2222   bilities, the RRWP positional features can be used to arbitrarily approximate several essential graph
2223   properties when fed into an MLP. Specifically, point 1 shows that RRWP with K − 1 steps encodes
2224
       all shortest path distances for nodes up to K − 1 hops. Additionally, points 2 and 3 indicate that
       RRWP features effectively capture diverse graph propagation dynamics.
2225
2226   Proposition 7 (Expressivity of an MLP with RRWP encoding (Ma et al., 2023)). For any n ∈ N,
2227   let Gn ⊆ {0, 1}n×n denote all adjacency matrices of n-node graphs. For K ∈ N, and A ∈ Gn ,
2228
       consider the RRWP:
2229                                 P = [I, M, . . . , M K−1 ] ∈ Rn×n×K
2230   Then, for any ϵ > 0, there exists an MLP : RK−1 → R acting independently across each n
2231   dimension such that MLP(P ) approximates any of the following to within ϵ error:
2232
2233         1. MLP(P )ij ≈ SPDK−1 (i, j)
2234                      PK−1
2235         2. MLP(P ) ≈ k=0 θk (D−1 A)k
2236
             3. MLP(P ) ≈ θ0 I + θ1 A
2237
2238   in which SPDK−1 (i, j) is the K − 1 truncated shortest path distance, and θk ∈ R are arbitrary
2239   coefficients.
2240
2241   Siraudin et al. (2024) experimentally validate the effectiveness of RRWP features for graph diffusion
2242   models and propose extending their proof to additional graph properties that GNNs fail to capture
2243   (Xu et al., 2019; Morris et al., 2019). For example, an MLP with input M k with k = N − 1 for an
2244   N -node graph can approximate the connected component of each node and the number of vertices in
2245
       the largest connected component. Additionally, RRWP features can be used to capture cycle-related
       information.
2246
2247
2248
       D.2.2    N ODE P ERMUTATION E QUIVARIANCE AND I NVARIANCE P ROPERTIES
2249   The different components of a graph generative model have to respect different graph symmetries.
2250   For example, the permutation equivariance of the model architecture ensures the output changes con-
2251   sistently with any reordering of input nodes, while permutation-invariant loss evaluates the model’s
2252   performance consistently across isomorphic graphs, regardless of node order. We provide a proof
2253   for related properties included in Lemma 1 as follows.
2254   Lemma 1 (Node Permutation Equivariance and Invariance Properties of DeFoG). The DeFoG model
2255   is permutation equivariant, its loss function is permutation invariant, and its sampling probability
2256   is permutation invariant.
2257
2258
       Proof. Recall that we denote an undirected graph with N nodes by G = (x1:n:N , e1:i<j:N ). Here,
2259
       each node variable is represented as xn ∈ X = {1, . . . , X}, and each edge variable as e(ij) ∈ E =
2260
       {1, . . . , E}. We also treat G as a multivariate data point consisting of D discrete variables including
2261
       all nodes and all edges.
2262
2263   We then consider a permutation function σ, which is applied to permute the graph’s node ordering.
2264   Under this permutation, the index n will be mapped to σ(n). We denote the ordered set of nodes
2265   and edges in the original ordering by x(1:n:N ) and e(1:i<j:N ) , respectively, and by x′(1:n:N ) and
2266   e′(1:i<j:N ) after permutation. Additionally, x(n) denotes the n-th entry of the corresponding ordered
2267
       set (and analogously for edges). By definition, the relationship between the original and permuted
                                                           −1                    −1      −1
       entries of the ordered sets is given by: x′(n) = xσ (n) and e′(ij) = e(σ (i),σ (j)) .


                                                          42
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

2270

2273                steps are defined as RRWP(M ) = P = [I, M, . . . , M K−1 ] ∈ Rn×n×K , where M k =
2274                (D−1 A)k , 0 ≤ k < K.
2275
2276

2302
2303   Permutation Invariant Loss Function DeFoG’s loss consists of summing the cross-entropy loss
2304   between the predicted clean graph and the true clean graph (node and edge-wise). Vignac et al.
2305   (2022), Lemma 3.2, provide a concise proof that this loss is permutation invariant.

2376
       E    C ONDITIONAL G ENERATION
2377
2378
       In this section, we describe how to seamlessly integrate DeFoG with existing methods for CTMC-
2379   based conditioning mechanisms. In this setting, all the examples are assumed to have a label. The
2380   objective of conditional generation is to steer the generative process based on that label, so that at
2381   sampling time we can guide the model to which class of samples we are interested in obtaining.
2382
       We focus on classifier-free guidance methods, as these models streamline training by avoiding task-
2383
       specific classifiers. This approach has been widely adopted for continuous state-space models, e.g.,
2384
       for image generation, where it has been shown to enhance the generation quality of the generative
2385   model (Ho & Salimans, 2021; Sanchez et al., 2024). Recently, Nisonoff et al. (2024) extended this
2386   method to discrete flow matching in a principled manner. In this paper, we adopt their formulation.
2387
2388
       In this framework, 90% of the training is performed with the model having access to the label of
       each noisy sample. This allows the model to learn the conditional rate matrix, Rtθ (zt , zt+dt |y). In the
2389
       remaining 10% of the training procedure, the labels of the samples are masked, forcing the model
2390
       to learn the unconditional generative rate matrix Rtθ (zt , zt+dt ). The conditional training enables
2391   targeted and accurate graph generation, while the unconditional phase ensures robustness when no
2392   conditions are specified. The combination of both conditional and unconditional training offers a
2393   more accurate pointer to the conditional distribution, typically described by the distance between
2394   the conditional and unconditional prediction. In our framework, this pointer is defined through the
2395   ratio between the conditional and unconditional rate matrices, as follows:
2396
2397                                                                                         γ−1
                                                                              Rtθ (x, x̃|y)
2398               Rtθ,γ (x, x̃|y) = Rtθ (x, x̃|y)γ Rtθ (x, x̃)1−γ = Rtθ (x, x̃|y)               ,
                                                                               Rtθ (x, x̃)
2399
2400   where γ denotes the guidance weight. In particular, the case with γ = 1 corresponds to standard
       conditional generation, while γ = 0 represents standard unconditional generation. As γ increases,
2401                                         θ           γ−1
                                             R (x,x̃|y)
2402   the conditioning effect described by Rt θ (x,x̃)      is strengthened, thereby enhancing the quality
                                                t
2403   of the generated samples. We observed γ = 2.0 to be the best performing value for our digital
2404   pathology experiments (Sec. 6.4), as detailed in Tab. 6.
2405
       Overall, conditional generation is pivotal for guiding models to produce graphs that meet specific
2406
       requirements, offering tailored solutions for complex real-world tasks. The flexibility of DeFoG,
2407
       being well-suited for conditional generation, marks an important step forward in advancing this
2408   direction, promising greater adaptability and precision in future graph-based applications.
2409
2410
2411
2412
2413
2414
2415
2416
2417
2418
2419
2420
2421
2422
2423
2424

2430
       F     E XPERIMENTAL D ETAILS
2431
2432
       This section provides further details on the experimental settings used in the paper.
2433
2434
2435   F.1     DATASET D ETAILS
2436
       F.1.1    S YNTHETIC DATASETS
2437
2438   Here, we describe the datasets employed in our experiments and outline the specific metrics used
2439   to evaluate model performance on each dataset. Additional visualizations of example graphs from
2440   each dataset, along with generated graphs, are provided in Figures 15 to 17.
2441
2442   Description We use three synthetic datasets with distinct topological structures. The first is the
2443   planar dataset (Martinkus et al., 2022), which consists of connected planar graphs—graphs that can
2444   be drawn on a plane without any edges crossing. The second dataset, tree (Bergmeister et al., 2023),
2445   contains tree graphs, which are connected graphs with no cycles. Lastly, the Stochastic Block Model
2446   (SBM) dataset (Martinkus et al., 2022) features synthetic clustering graphs where nodes within the
2447   same cluster have a higher probability of being connected.
2448   The planar and tree datasets exhibit well-defined deterministic graph structures, while the SBM
2449   dataset, commonly used in the literature, stands out due to its stochasticity, resulting from the random
2450   sampling process that governs its connectivity.
2451
2452   Metrics We follow the evaluation procedures described by Martinkus et al. (2022); Bergmeister
2453   et al. (2023), using both dataset-agnostic and dataset-specific metrics.
2454
       First, dataset-agnostic metrics assess the alignment between the generated and training distributions
2455
       for specific general graph properties. We map the graphs to their node degrees (Deg.), clustering
2456
       coefficients (Clus.), orbit count (Orbit), eigenvalues of the normalized graph Laplacian (Spec.), and
2457   statistics derived from a wavelet graph transform (Wavelet). We then compute the distance to the
2458   corresponding statistics calculated for the test graphs. For each statistic, we measure the distance
2459   between the empirical distributions of the generated and test sets using Maximum Mean Discrepancy
2460   (MMD). These distances are aggregated into the Ratio metric. To compute this, we first calculate
2461   the MMD distances between the training and test sets for the same graph statistics. The final Ratio
2462   metric is obtained by dividing the average MMD distance between the generated and test sets by
2463   the average MMD distance between the training and test sets. A Ratio value of 1 is ideal, as the
2464   distance between the training and test sets represents a lower-bound reference for the generated
2465
       data’s performance.
2466   Next, we report dataset-specific metrics using the V.U.N. framework, which assesses the proportion
2467   of graphs that are valid (V), unique (U), and novel (N). Validity is assessed based on dataset-specific
2468   properties: the graph must be planar, a tree, or statistically consistent with an SBM for the planar,
2469   tree, and SBM datasets, respectively. Uniqueness captures the proportion of non-isomorphic graphs
2470   within the generated graphs, while novelty measures how many of these graphs are non-isomorphic
2471
       to any graph in the training set.
2472
2473   F.1.2    M OLECULAR DATASETS
2474
       Description Molecular generation is a key real-world application of graph generation. It poses
2475
       a challenging task to current graph generation models to their rich chemistry-specific information,
2476
       involving several nodes and edges classes and leaning how to generate them jointly, and more com-
2477   plex evaluation pipelines. To assess DeFoG’s performance on molecular datasets, we use three
2478   benchmarks that progressively increase in molecular complexity and size.
2479
2480
       First, we use the QM9 dataset (Wu et al., 2018), a subset of GDB9 (Ruddigkeit et al., 2012), which
       contains molecules with up to 9 heavy atoms.
2481
2482   Next, we evaluate DeFoG on the Moses benchmark (Polykovskiy et al., 2020), derived from the
2483   ZINC Clean Leads collection (Sterling & Irwin, 2015), featuring molecules with 8 to 27 heavy
       atoms, filtered by specific criteria.


                                                         46
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2484
       Finally, we include the Guacamol benchmark (Brown et al., 2019), based on the ChEMBL 24
2485   database (Mendez et al., 2019). This dataset comprises synthesized molecules, tested against bi-
2486   ological targets, with sizes ranging from 2 to 88 heavy atoms.
2487
2488
       Next, we evaluate DeFoG on the Moses benchmark (Polykovskiy et al., 2020), derived from the
       ZINC Clean Leads collection (Sterling & Irwin, 2015), which contains molecules ranging from 8 to
2489
       27 heavy atoms, filtered according to certain criteria. Lastly, we include the Guacamol benchmark
2490
       (Brown et al., 2019), derived from the ChEMBL 24 database (Mendez et al., 2019). This benchmark
2491   contains only molecules that have been synthesized and tested against biological targets, with 2 to
2492   88 heavy atoms.
2493
2494
       Metrics For the QM9 dataset, we follow the dataset splits and evaluation metrics outlined by
2495
       Vignac et al. (2022). For the Moses and Guacamol benchmarks, we adhere to the training setups
2496
       and evaluation metrics proposed by Polykovskiy et al. (2020) and Brown et al. (2019), respectively.
2497   Note that Guacamol includes molecules with charges; therefore, the generated graphs are converted
2498   to charged molecules based on the relaxed validity criterion used by Jo et al. (2022) before being
2499   translated to their corresponding SMILES representations. The validity, uniqueness, and novelty
2500   metrics reported by the Guacamol benchmark are actually V, V.U., and V.U.N., and are referred to
2501   directly as V, V.U., and V.U.N. in the table for clarity.
2502
2503   F.1.3    D IGITAL PATHOLOGY DATASETS
2504
2505   Description Graphs, with their natural ability to represent relational data, are widely used to cap-
2506   ture spatial biological dependencies in tissue images. This approach has proven successful in digital
2507   pathology tasks such as microenvironment classification (Wu et al., 2022), cancer classification (Pati
2508   et al., 2022), and decision explainability (Jaume et al., 2021). More recently, graph-based methods
2509
       have been applied to generative tasks (Madeira et al., 2023), and an open-source dataset was made
       available by Madeira et al. (2024). This dataset consists of cell graphs where the nodes represent
2510
       biological cells, categorized into 9 distinct cell types (node classes), and edges model local cell-cell
2511
       interactions (a single class). For further details, refer to Madeira et al. (2024).
2512
2513
2514   Metrics Each cell graph in the dataset can be mapped to a TLS (Tertiary Lymphoid Structure)
2515
       embedding, denoted as κ = [κ0 , . . . , κ5 ] ∈ R6 , which quantifies its TLS content. A graph G is
       classified as having low TLS content if κ1 (G) < 0.05, and high TLS content if κ2 (G) > 0.05.
2516
       Based on these criteria, the dataset is split into two subsets: high TLS and low TLS. In prior work,
2517
       TLS generation accuracy was evaluated by training generative models on these subsets separately,
2518   and verifying if the generated graphs matched the corresponding TLS content label. We compute
2519   TLS accuracy as the average accuracy across both subsets. For DeFoG, we conditionally train it
2520   on both subsets simultaneously, as described in Appendix E, and compute TLS accuracy based on
2521   whether the generated graphs adhere to the conditioning label. Additionally, we report the V.U.N.
2522   metric (valid, unique, novel), similar to what is done for the synthetic datasets (see Appendix F.1.1).
2523   A graph is considered valid in this case if it is a connected planar graph, as the graphs in these
2524   datasets were constructed using Delaunay triangulation.
2525
2526   F.2     R ESOURCES
2527
2528   The training and sampling times for the different datasets explored in this paper are provided in
2529   Tab. 5. All the experiments in this work were run on a single NVIDIA A100-SXM4-80GB GPU.
2530
2531   F.3     H YPERPARAMETER T UNING
2532
2533   The default hyperparameters for training and sampling for each dataset can be found in the pro-
2534   vided code repository. In Tab. 6, we specifically highlight their values for the proposed training
2535   (see Sec. 4.2.1) and sampling (see Sec. 4.2.2) strategies, and conditional guidance parameter (see
2536   Appendix E). As the training process is by far the most computationally costly stage, we aim to
2537   minimize changes to the default model training configuration. Nevertheless, we demonstrate the
       effectiveness of these modifications on certain datasets:


                                                         47
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

                   SBM                     44              187                  75                     40                       0.07

                   QM9                     2                 9                  6.5                  10000                      0.2

                   Guacamol                2                88                  141                  10000                       7

2549   and molecular datasets respectively).

2551                                 Train                                                      Sampling                                    Conditional
2552    Dataset     Initial Distribution     Train Distortion    Sample Distortion    ω (Target Guidance)   η (Stochasticity)   Exact Exp       γ
2553    Planar          Marginal                Identity             Polydec                 0.05                 50              True          —
        Tree            Marginal                Polydec              Polydec                  0.0                 0.0             False         —
2554    SBM             Absorbing               Identity             Identity                 0.0                 0.0             False         —
        QM9             Marginal                Identity             Polydec                  0.0                 0.0             False         —
2555    QM9(H)          Marginal                Identity             Polydec                 0.05                 0.0             False         —

2558
2559
2560          1. SBM performs particularly well with absorbing distributions, likely due to its distinct clus-
2561             tering structure, which differs from other graph properties. Additionally, when tested with
2562             a marginal model, SBM can achieve a V.U.N. of 80.5% and an average ratio of 2.5, which
2563             also reaches state-of-the-art performance.
2564          2. Guacamol and MOSES are trained directly with polydec distortion to accelerate conver-
2565             gence, as these datasets are very large and typically require a significantly longer training
2566             period.
2567          3. For the tree dataset, standard training yielded suboptimal results (85.3% for V.U.N. and 1.8
2568             for average ratio). However, a quick re-training using polydec distortion achieved state-of-
2569             the-art performance with 7 hours of training.

       G.2   M OLECULAR G RAPH G ENERATION
2809
2810   For the molecular generation tasks, we begin by examining the results for QM9, considering both
2811   implicit and explicit hydrogens (Vignac et al., 2022). In the implicit case, hydrogen atoms are
2812   inferred to complete the valencies, while in the explicit case, hydrogens must be explicitly modeled,
2813   making it an inherently more challenging task. The results are presented in Tab. 8. Notably, DeFoG
2814   achieves training set validity in both scenarios, representing the theoretical maximum. Furthermore,
2815   DeFoG consistently outperforms other models in terms of FCD. Remarkably, even with only 10%
2816
       of the sampling steps, DeFoG surpasses many existing methods.

       G.3                          I MPACT OF A DDITIONAL F EATURES
2872
2873
2874   In graph diffusion methods, the task of graph generation is decomposed into a mapping of a graph
2875
       to a set of marginal probabilities for each node and edge. This problem is typically addressed using
       a Graph Transformer architecture, which is augmented with additional features to capture structural
2876
       aspects that the base architecture might struggle to model effectively (Vignac et al., 2022; Xu et al.,
2877
       2024; Siraudin et al., 2024) otherwise. In this section, we evaluate the impact of using RRWP
2878   encodings as opposed to the spectral and cycle encodings (up to 6-cycles) proposed in DiGress
2879   (Vignac et al., 2022).
2880
2881
       In Tab. 11, we present a performance comparison of these two variants across three datasets: QM9,
       Planar, and SBM. The results show that RRWP achieves comparable or superior performance within
2882
       the DiGress framework, validating its effectiveness as a graph encoding method. Notably, despite
2883
       these improvements, DiGress’s performance remains significantly below that of DeFoG on the Pla-
2884   nar and SBM datasets, while achieving similar validity and uniqueness on the QM9 dataset.
2885
2886   To further demonstrate the impact of using RRWP on sampling efficiency, we compare the perfor-
2887
       mances of DeFoG, DiGress, and DiGress augmented with RRWP (replacing the original additional
       features) across a varying number of sampling steps. These results are shown in Figure 18.

2914   We observe that, while RRWP provides improvements on the Planar and SBM datasets with fewer
2915   generation steps, it is still significantly outperformed by the optimized DeFoG framework. This
       highlights that although RRWP is an efficient and effective graph encoding method, the primary


                                                                                                 54
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2916   Table 12: Computation time for different additional features. The RRWP features are computed
2917   with 12 steps.
2918
2919                         Dataset     Min Nodes     Max Nodes   RRWP (ms)          Cycles (ms)         Spectral (ms)
2920                         Moses            8            27             0.6                   1.2            2.5
                             Planar          64            64             0.5                   1.2           93.0
2921                         SBM             44           187             0.6                   2.7          146.9
2922
2923
2924   performance gains of DeFoG stem from its continuous-time formulation featuring fully decoupled
2925   training and sampling stages.
2926
       We then perform a time complexity analysis of these methods. While both cycle and RRWP encod-
2927
       ings primarily involve matrix multiplications, spectral encodings require more complex algorithms
2928   for eigenvalue and eigenvector computation. As shown in Tab. 12, cycle and RRWP encodings
2929   are more computationally efficient, particularly for larger graphs where eigenvalue computation be-
2930   comes increasingly costly. These results also support the use of RRWP encodings over the combined
2931   utilization of cycle and spectral features.
2932
       For the graph sizes considered in this work, the additional feature computation time remains rela-
2933
       tively small compared to the model’s forward pass and backpropagation. However, as graph sizes
2934   increase - a direction beyond the scope of this paper - this computational gap could become signifi-
2935   cant, making RRWP a suitable encoding for scalable graph generative models.
2936
2937   G.4    C OMPUTATION C OST FOR E XACT E XPECTATION OF R ATE M ATRIX
2938
2939   The proposed denoising process iteratively applies the dimension-independent
                                                                                           Euler step
                                                                                                      described
2940
                                                                                         d
       in Eq. (4). This requires computing the expectation Epθ,d (zd |z1:D ) Rtd (ztd , zt+∆t |z1d ) . Two proce-
                                                                                1|t   1     t
2941   dures are available for this computation: an approximated version, by sequential sampling of con-
2942   ditional distributions, as suggested by Campbell et al. (2024), and an exact version, by evaluating a
2943   weighted sum over possible rate matrices:
2944                                                             X θ,d d 1:D d d d
2945                  Epθ,d (zd |z1:D ) Rtd (ztd , zt+∆t

2947
       This exact computation involves a sweep over all possible states of z1d . For graphs, this corre-
2948
       sponds to iterating over all possible node and edge states at each denoising step. However, since
2949
       the denoising neural network computes pθ,d             d
                                                 1|t for all z1 in a single forward pass (outputting marginal
2950
       distributions for each node and edge), the exact expectation does not incur additional network calls.
2951
2952   In our experiments, the cardinalities of the node (X) and edge (E) state spaces are relatively small:
2953
2954          • Synthetic datasets: X = 1, E = 2;
2955          • Molecular datasets: X = 4, E = 5;
2956          • Digital Pathology datasets: X = 2, E = 9.
2957
2958   Tab. 13 summarizes the runtimes for representative datasets. These results show that the computa-
2959   tional overhead of the exact expectation in our setting is minimal. Importantly, as anticipated, the
2960   overhead increases with the cardinalities of the state spaces.
